<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>The Washington Post</i></h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"WaPo" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/WAPO_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="WAPO (disambiguation)">WAPO (disambiguation)</a>.</div> 
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Washington Post" and "The Washington Post Times Herald" redirect here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Washington_Post_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Washington Post (disambiguation)">Washington Post (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="plainlist" style="line-height:1.3em; padding-top:0.4em; border-top: 1px solid #aaa"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_United_States" title="Media of the United States">Media of the United States</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States" title="List of newspapers in the United States">List of newspapers</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>The Washington Post</b></i> is a major American <a href="/wiki/Daily_newspaper" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily newspaper">daily newspaper</a> founded on December 6, 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It is the largest newspaper published in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, the capital city of the United States, (and <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States#Top_25_newspapers_by_circulation,_late_2012_through_early_2013" title="List of newspapers in the United States">8th of the US</a>) and has a particular emphasis on national politics. Its slogan "Democracy Dies in Darkness" appears on its <a href="/wiki/Masthead_(publishing)" title="Masthead (publishing)">masthead</a>. Daily <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> editions are printed for the <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>.
</p><p>The newspaper has won <a href="/wiki/List_of_prizes_won_by_The_Washington_Post" title="List of prizes won by The Washington Post">47 Pulitzer Prizes</a>. This includes six separate <a href="/wiki/2008_Pulitzer_Prize" title="2008 Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzers awarded in 2008</a>, second only to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span> seven awards in <a href="/wiki/2002_Pulitzer_Prize" title="2002 Pulitzer Prize">2002</a> for the highest number ever awarded to a single newspaper in one year.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <i>Post</i> journalists have also received 18 <a href="/wiki/Nieman_Fellowship" title="Nieman Fellowship">Nieman Fellowships</a> and 368 White House News Photographers Association awards. In the early 1970s, in the best-known episode in the newspaper's history, reporters <a href="/wiki/Bob_Woodward" title="Bob Woodward">Bob Woodward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bernstein" title="Carl Bernstein">Carl Bernstein</a> led the American press' investigation into what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate scandal</a>; reporting in the newspaper greatly contributed to the resignation of President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>. In years since, its investigations have led to increased review of the <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Medical_Center" title="Walter Reed Army Medical Center">Walter Reed Army Medical Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2013, the paper's longtime controlling family, the <a href="/wiki/Donald_E._Graham" title="Donald E. Graham">Graham family</a>, sold the newspaper to <a href="/wiki/Nash_Holdings" class="mw-redirect" title="Nash Holdings">Nash Holdings</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Holding_company" title="Holding company">holding company</a> established by <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a>, for $250 million in cash.<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Post_building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Washington_Post_building.jpg/220px-Washington_Post_building.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Washington_Post_building.jpg/330px-Washington_Post_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Washington_Post_building.jpg/440px-Washington_Post_building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Post_building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The previous headquarters of <i>The Washington Post</i> on 15th Street NW in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div></div></div>
<p><i>The Washington Post</i> is regarded as one of the leading daily American newspapers,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> along with <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>. The <i>Post</i> has distinguished itself through its <a href="/wiki/Political_journalism" title="Political journalism">political reporting</a> on the workings of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congress</a>, and other aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. government</a>.
</p><p>Unlike <i>The New York Times</i> and <i>The Wall Street Journal</i>, <i>The Washington Post</i> does not print an edition for distribution away from the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">East Coast</a>. In 2009, the newspaper ceased publication of its <i>National Weekly Edition</i>, which combined stories from the week's print editions, due to shrinking circulation.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The majority of its newsprint readership is in the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a> and its suburbs in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Virginia" title="Northern Virginia">Northern Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The newspaper is one of a few U.S. newspapers with <a href="/wiki/News_bureau" title="News bureau">foreign bureaus</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamabad" title="Islamabad">Islamabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In November 2009, it announced the closure of its U.S. regional bureaus—Chicago, Los Angeles and New York—as part of an increased focus on "...political stories and local news coverage in Washington."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper has local bureaus in Maryland (<a href="/wiki/Annapolis,_Maryland" title="Annapolis, Maryland">Annapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Maryland" title="Montgomery County, Maryland">Montgomery County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_George%27s_County,_Maryland" title="Prince George&#39;s County, Maryland">Prince George's County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Maryland" title="Southern Maryland">Southern Maryland</a>) and Virginia (<a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fairfax,_Virginia" title="Fairfax, Virginia">Fairfax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loudoun_County,_Virginia" title="Loudoun County, Virginia">Loudoun County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prince_William_County,_Virginia" title="Prince William County, Virginia">Prince William County</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  May&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, its average weekday circulation was 474,767, according to the <a href="/wiki/Audit_Bureau_of_Circulations_(North_America)" class="mw-redirect" title="Audit Bureau of Circulations (North America)">Audit Bureau of Circulations</a>, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States_by_circulation" class="mw-redirect" title="List of newspapers in the United States by circulation">seventh largest newspaper in the country by circulation</a>, behind <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_News_(New_York)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily News (New York)">Daily News</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Post" title="New York Post">New York Post</a></i>. While its circulation (like that of almost all newspapers) has been slipping, it has one of the highest market-penetration rates of any metropolitan news daily.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For many decades, the <i>Post</i> had its main office at 1150 15th Street NW. This real estate remained with Graham Holdings when the newspaper was sold to Jeff Bezos' Nash Holdings in 2013. Graham Holdings sold 1150 15th Street (along with 1515 L Street, 1523 L Street, and land beneath 1100 15th Street) for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$159<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million</span> in November 2013. <i>The Washington Post</i> continued to lease space at 1150 L Street NW.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In May 2014, <i>The Washington Post</i> leased the west tower of <a href="/wiki/One_Franklin_Square" title="One Franklin Square">One Franklin Square</a>, a <a href="/wiki/High-rise" class="mw-redirect" title="High-rise">high-rise</a> building at <a href="/wiki/K_Street_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="K Street (Washington, D.C.)">1301 K Street NW</a> in Washington, D.C. The newspaper moved into their new offices December 14, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Publishing_service">Publishing service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Publishing service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Arc Publishing is a department of the Post, which provides the publishing system, Arc, software for news organizations such as the <i>Chicago Tribune</i> and the <i>Los Angeles Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_early_period">Founding and early period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Founding and early period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sign,_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters,%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington,_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg/220px-Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg/330px-Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg/440px-Sign%2C_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters%2C%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington%2C_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2390" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sign,_%22Welcome_Home_From_the_Crow-Eaters,%22_on_the_front_of_the_Washington_Post_Building_in_Washington,_DC._President..._-_NARA_-_199955.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Washington Post</i> building in 1948</div></div></div>
<p>The newspaper was founded in 1877 by <a href="/wiki/Stilson_Hutchins" title="Stilson Hutchins">Stilson Hutchins</a> (1838–1912) and in 1880 added a Sunday edition, becoming the city's first newspaper to publish seven days a week. In 1889, Hutchins sold the newspaper to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Hatton_(U.S._politician)" title="Frank Hatton (U.S. politician)">Frank Hatton</a>, a former Postmaster General, and <a href="/wiki/Beriah_Wilkins" title="Beriah Wilkins">Beriah Wilkins</a>, a former Democratic congressman from Ohio. To promote the newspaper, the new owners requested the leader of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Band" title="United States Marine Band">United States Marine Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Philip_Sousa" title="John Philip Sousa">John Philip Sousa</a>, to compose a <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">march</a> for the newspaper's essay contest awards ceremony. Sousa composed "<a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post_(march)" title="The Washington Post (march)">The Washington Post</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> It became the standard music to accompany the <a href="/wiki/Two-step_(dance_move)" title="Two-step (dance move)">two-step</a>, a late 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Dance_craze" class="mw-redirect" title="Dance craze">dance craze</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and remains one of Sousa's best-known works.
</p><p>In 1893, the newspaper moved to a building at 14th and E streets NW, where it would remain until 1950. This building combined all functions of the newspaper into one headquarters – newsroom, advertising, typesetting, and printing – that ran 24 hours per day.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodbye_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodbye-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1898, during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>, the <i>Post</i> printed <a href="/wiki/Clifford_K._Berryman" title="Clifford K. Berryman">Clifford K. Berryman</a>'s classic illustration <i><a href="/wiki/Remember_the_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Remember the Maine">Remember the Maine</a></i>, which became the battle-cry for American sailors during the War. In 1902, Berryman published another famous cartoon in the <i>Post</i>—<i>Drawing the Line in Mississippi</i>. This cartoon depicts President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> showing compassion for a small bear cub and inspired New York store owner <a href="/wiki/Morris_Michtom" title="Morris Michtom">Morris Michtom</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/Teddy_bear" title="Teddy bear">teddy bear</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wilkins acquired Hatton's share of the newspaper in 1894 at Hatton's death. After Wilkins' death in 1903, his sons John and Robert ran the <i>Post</i> for two years before selling it in 1905 to <a href="/wiki/John_Roll_McLean" class="mw-redirect" title="John Roll McLean">John Roll McLean</a>, owner of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Enquirer" class="mw-redirect" title="Cincinnati Enquirer">Cincinnati Enquirer</a></i>. During the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Wilson</a> presidency, the <i>Post</i> was credited with the "most famous newspaper <a href="/wiki/Typo" class="mw-redirect" title="Typo">typo</a>" in D.C. history according to <i><a href="/wiki/Reason_(magazine)" title="Reason (magazine)">Reason</a></i> magazine; the <i>Post</i> intended to report that President Wilson had been "entertaining" his future-wife Mrs. Galt, but instead wrote that he had been "entering" Mrs. Galt.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tws5nov01_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tws5nov01-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tws5nov02_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tws5nov02-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>When John McLean died in 1916, he put the newspaper in trust, having little faith that his playboy son <a href="/wiki/Edward_Beale_McLean" title="Edward Beale McLean">Edward "Ned" McLean</a> could manage his inheritance. Ned went to court and broke the trust, but, under his management, the newspaper slumped toward ruin. He bled the paper for his lavish lifestyle, and used it to promote political agendas.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Meyer.E2.80.93Graham_period"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Meyer–Graham_period">Meyer–Graham period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Meyer–Graham period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(November 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 1929, <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">Wall Street</a> financier <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Meyer_(financier)" title="Eugene Meyer (financier)">Eugene Meyer</a> secretly made an offer of $5 million for the <i>Post,</i> but he was rebuffed by Ned McLean.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On June 1, 1933, Meyer bought the paper at a bankruptcy auction for $825,000 three weeks after stepping down as <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman of the Federal Reserve">Chairman of the Federal Reserve</a>. He had bid anonymously, and was prepared to go up to $2 million, far higher than the other bidders.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> These included <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>, who had long hoped to shut down the ailing <i>Post</i>  to benefit his own Washington newspaper presence.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The <i>Post's</i> health and reputation were restored under Meyer's ownership. In 1946, he was succeeded as publisher by his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Graham" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Graham">Philip Graham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Meyer eventually got the last laugh over Hearst, who had owned the old <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Times_(1894%E2%80%931939)" title="Washington Times (1894–1939)">Washington Times</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Herald" title="Washington Herald">Herald</a></i> before their 1939 merger that formed the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Times-Herald" title="Washington Times-Herald">Times-Herald</a>.</i> This was in turn bought by and merged into the <i>Post</i> in 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The combined paper was officially named <i>The Washington Post and Times-Herald</i> until 1973, although the <i>Times-Herald</i> portion of the <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_(publishing)" title="Nameplate (publishing)">nameplate</a> became less and less prominent over time. The merger left the <i>Post</i> with two remaining local competitors, the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Star" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Star">Washington Star</a></i> (<i>Evening Star</i>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Daily_News" title="Washington Daily News">Washington Daily News</a></i> which merged together in 1972, forming the <i>Washington Star-News.</i><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This had again become simply the <i>Washington Star</i> by the time it closed on August 7, 1981, leaving the <i>Post</i> as the only major daily in Washington for almost a year.
On May 17, 1982, <a href="/wiki/Unification_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification Church">Unification Church</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Sun_Myung_Moon" title="Sun Myung Moon">Sun Myung Moon</a> began publishing the current <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Times">Washington Times</a></i>, a conservative daily <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> which has always had a circulation only a fraction of the <i>Post's.</i> In 2005, conservative competition increased slightly with the founding of the free <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a> daily <a href="/wiki/Washington_Examiner" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Examiner"><i>The Washington Examiner</i></a>, which switched to a weekly magazine format in 2013. But the <i>Post</i>, with a far larger presence locally and now nationally as well, has remained Washington's dominant paper since the 1960s. 
</p><p><br />
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg/220px-Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg/330px-Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg/440px-Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2356" data-file-height="2795" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Land_on_the_Moon_7_21_1969-repair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Monday, July 21, 1969, edition, with the headline <span style="padding-right:.2em;">"</span>'The Eagle Has Landed'&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;<a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Two Men Walk on the Moon</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>After Phil Graham's death in 1963, control of The Washington Post Company passed to his wife <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Graham" title="Katharine Graham">Katharine Graham</a> (1917–2001), who was also Eugene Meyer's daughter. Few women had run nationally prominent newspapers in the United States. Katharine Graham described her own anxiety and lack of confidence based on her gender in her autobiography. She served as publisher from 1969 to 1979<sup id="cite_ref-:0_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and headed The Washington Post Company into the early 1990s as chairman of the board and CEO. After 1993, she retained a position as chairman of the executive committee until her death in 2001.
</p><p>Her tenure is credited with seeing the newspaper rise in national stature through effective investigative reporting after it began to live down its reputation as a house organ for the Kennedy and Johnson administration, working to ensure that <i>The New York Times</i> did not surpass its Washington reporting of the <a href="/wiki/Pentagon_Papers" title="Pentagon Papers">Pentagon Papers</a> and Watergate scandal.
</p><p>Graham took The Washington Post Company public on June 15, 1971 in the midst of the Pentagon Papers controversy. A total of 1,294,000 shares were offered to the public at $26 per share.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> By the end of Graham's tenure as CEO in 1991, the stock was worth $888 per share, not counting the effect of an intermediate 4:1 stock split.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time, Graham also oversaw the Post company's diversification purchase of the for-profit education and training company <a href="/wiki/Kaplan,_Inc." title="Kaplan, Inc.">Kaplan, Inc.</a> for $40 million in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Twenty years later, Kaplan had surpassed the Post newspaper as the company's leading contributor to income, and by 2010 Kaplan accounted for more than 60% of the entire company revenue stream.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Executive editor <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bradlee" title="Ben Bradlee">Ben Bradlee</a>, a Kennedy loyalist, put the newspaper's reputation and resources behind reporters <a href="/wiki/Bob_Woodward" title="Bob Woodward">Bob Woodward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bernstein" title="Carl Bernstein">Carl Bernstein</a>, who, in a long series of articles, chipped away at the story behind the 1972 burglary of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_National_Committee" title="Democratic National Committee">Democratic National Committee</a> offices in the <a href="/wiki/Watergate_complex" title="Watergate complex">Watergate complex</a> in Washington. The <i>Post</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> dogged coverage of the story, the outcome of which ultimately played a major role in the resignation of President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, won the newspaper a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> in 1973.
</p><p>In 1972, the "Book World" section was introduced with Pulitzer Prize–winning critic <a href="/wiki/William_McPherson_(writer)" title="William McPherson (writer)">William McPherson</a> as its first editor.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> It featured Pulitzer Prize–winning critics such as <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Yardley" title="Jonathan Yardley">Jonathan Yardley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dirda" title="Michael Dirda">Michael Dirda</a>, the latter of whom established his career as a critic at the <i>Post</i>. In 2009, after 37 years, with great reader outcries and protest, <i>The Washington Post Book World</i> as a standalone insert was discontinued, the last issue being Sunday, February 15, 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> along with a general reorganization of the paper, such as placing the Sunday editorials on the back page of the main front section rather than the "Outlook" section and distributing some other locally oriented "op-ed" letters and commentaries in other sections.<sup id="cite_ref-lastbook_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lastbook-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> However, book reviews are still published in the Outlook section on Sundays and in the Style section the rest of the week, as well as online.<sup id="cite_ref-lastbook_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lastbook-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980, the newspaper published a dramatic story called "Jimmy's World",<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> describing the life of an eight-year-old <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a> addict in Washington, for which reporter <a href="/wiki/Janet_Cooke" title="Janet Cooke">Janet Cooke</a> won acclaim and a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>. Subsequent investigation, however, revealed the story to be a fabrication. The Pulitzer Prize was returned.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Donald_E._Graham" title="Donald E. Graham">Donald E. Graham</a>, Katharine's son, succeeded her as publisher in 1979<sup id="cite_ref-:0_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and in the early 1990s became both chief executive officer and chairman of the board. He was succeeded in 2000 as publisher and CEO by <a href="/wiki/Boisfeuillet_Jones_Jr." title="Boisfeuillet Jones Jr.">Boisfeuillet Jones Jr.</a>, with Graham remaining as chairman.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Katharine_Weymouth" title="Katharine Weymouth">Katharine Weymouth</a>, Donald Graham's niece, served as publisher and chief executive officer from 2008 until 2014, when Jeff Bezos took over ownership of the paper.
</p>
<h3><span id="Jeff_Bezos_era_.282013.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jeff_Bezos_era_(2013–present)">Jeff Bezos era (2013–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Jeff Bezos era (2013–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a> purchased the newspaper for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$250<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million</span> in cash, completing the transaction on October 1, 2013, after announcing the planned <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">acquisition</a> on August 5, 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-fahri2013a_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahri2013a-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-clabaugh2013_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clabaugh2013-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fahri2013_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahri2013-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper is currently owned by Nash Holdings LLC, a holding company created for the acquisition and controlled by Bezos.<sup id="cite_ref-fahri2013a_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahri2013a-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sale included the Spanish language newspaper <i>El Tiempo Latino</i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Fairfax_Times" title="Fairfax Times">Fairfax Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gazette_(Maryland)" title="The Gazette (Maryland)">The Gazette</a></i>, the free daily newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Express_(Washington,_D.C._newspaper)" title="Express (Washington, D.C. newspaper)">Express</a></i>, Southern Maryland Newspapers, and several newspapers covering and for the U.S. armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-NashHoldingsincludes_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NashHoldingsincludes-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nash Holdings also took ownership of the <i>Post</i> printing plants in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Virginia" title="Springfield, Virginia">Springfield, Virginia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fairfax_County,_Virginia" title="Fairfax County, Virginia">Fairfax County, Virginia</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Laurel,_Maryland" title="Laurel, Maryland">Laurel, Maryland</a> (the "Comprint plant").<sup id="cite_ref-NashHoldingsincludes_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NashHoldingsincludes-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Form8K_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Form8K-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Other assets included in the sale were the publications <i>Apartment Showcase</i>, <i>Capital Business</i>, <i>Fashion Washington</i>, <i>Guide to Retirement Living Sourcebook</i>, <i>New Condominium Guide</i>, and <i>New Homes Guide</i>; the Internet sites TheCapitolDeal.com and ServiceAlley.com; and Comprint Military Publications (which included eight weekly newspapers covering local military bases, 10 annual guides to local military bases, and the Web sites DCMilitary.com, DCMilitaryEd.com, DCMilitaryFamLife.com).<sup id="cite_ref-Form8K_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Form8K-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Some real estate was also included in the deal, such as a one-story office building in <a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_County,_Maryland" title="St. Mary&#39;s County, Maryland">St. Mary's County, Maryland</a>; warehouses in Fairfax County, Virginia; two tracts of land in Fairfax County, Virginia; leased office space in <a href="/wiki/Charles_County,_Maryland" title="Charles County, Maryland">Charles County, Maryland</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Maryland" title="Montgomery County, Maryland">Montgomery County, Maryland</a>; and 23 acres of undeveloped land in Charles County, Maryland.<sup id="cite_ref-Form8K_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Form8K-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Not included in the sale were other Washington Post Company assets, including the Washington Post Company's downtown office building, the Post's Robinson Terminal facilities in Alexandria, Virginia; Post-Newsweek Stations; Cable ONE (a <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a>-based Internet and cable service provider); independent web-based media assets such as Slate Group (<i><a href="/wiki/Slate_(magazine)" title="Slate (magazine)">Slate</a></i> magazine and its sister video magazine, Slate V), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Root_(magazine)" title="The Root (magazine)">The Root</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Policy (magazine)">Foreign Policy</a></i>; social media marketing company Social Code; home healthcare and hospice provider Celtic Healthcare; and the energy parts supplier Forney Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RenamedGraham_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RenamedGraham-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> After the completion of the sale, a press release announced the name change of the Washington Post Company to Graham Holdings Company (the change was made effective on November 29, 2013).<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RenamedGraham_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RenamedGraham-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In early September 2013, Bezos summarized his approach for the news organization—with a vision that recreates "the 'daily ritual' of reading the <i>Post</i> as a bundle, not merely a series of individual stories"&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;although he indicated that the experience was more likely to be created on <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computers</a> and less likely "on the Web".<sup id="cite_ref-wp20130904_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wp20130904-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Bezos has been described as a "hands-off owner", holding teleconference calls with executive editor Martin Baron every two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2014, <i>The Washington Post</i> launched "Get There", an online personal finance section.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014, Bezos announced his decision to appoint <a href="/wiki/Fred_Ryan" title="Fred Ryan">Frederick J. Ryan Jr.</a>, founding President and CEO of <a href="/wiki/Politico" title="Politico">Politico</a>, to serve as Publisher and CEO of <i>The Washington Post</i>, effective October 1, 2014. This signaled Bezos’ intent to shift <i>The Post</i> to a more digital focus with a strategy for expanding to a broader national and global readership. Ryan has continued to invest in news and technology while reducing expenses in legacy print areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Fred_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fred-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg/220px-Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg/330px-Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg/440px-Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Post_demolition_2016.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demolition of the previous headquarters in April 2016</div></div></div>
<p>Nash Holdings divested itself of a number of newspapers, and closed two others, in the summer of 2015. The company announced on June 12, 2015, that it would close the <i>Montgomery Gazette</i> and <i>Prince George's Gazette</i> effective June 18, 2015. The company also sold Comprint Military Publications and its Southern Maryland Newspapers group (which consisted of the <i>Maryland Independent</i>, <i>The Enterprise</i>, the <i>Calvert Recorder</i>, and the <i>Enquirer Gazette</i>, and their associated Southern Maryland Newspaper Web site) to Adams Publishing Group. The company also said it would sell the <i>Fairfax County Times</i> to Whip It Media, a locally owned company founded by the <i>Times</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span> former general manager, Richard Whippen.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2014, the <i>Post</i> announced it was moving into new headquarters space at <a href="/wiki/One_Franklin_Square" title="One Franklin Square">One Franklin Square</a> in December 2015. The company leased 242,000 square feet (22,500&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of space for 16 years on floors four through nine in the west tower and floors seven and eight in the east tower. The building's owner agreed to an extensive build-out: Only about 10 percent of the space will be private offices, which required extensive demolition of interior walls and the removal of the walls on the seventh and eighth floor in the east tower so they joined with the floors on the west tower. The newly joined space will create two 60,000-square-foot (5,600&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) floors capable of accommodating 700 newsroom workers and software engineers. The build-out includes four sets for live television filming, a new staircase between the seventh and eighth floors in each tower, and a two-story auditorium on the fourth floor. The building's south-facing facade will also be altered to give <i>Post</i> workers floor-to-ceiling windows.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 3, 2017, the <i>Post</i> launched a new history blog, Retropolis, that features daily posts aimed at connecting present-day news with its rich history.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 12, 2018, the <i>Post</i> began a podcast called Retropod which is a show for history lovers, featuring stories about the past which rediscover forgotten heroes, overlooked villains, dreamers and more.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_stance">Political stance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Political stance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="1933.E2.80.932000"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1933–2000">1933–2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1933–2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When financier <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Meyer_(financier)" title="Eugene Meyer (financier)">Eugene Meyer</a> bought the bankrupt <i>Post</i> in 1933, he assured the public he wouldn't be beholden to any party.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> But as a Republican (it was <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> who had made him <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman of the Federal Reserve">Fed Chairman</a> in 1930), his opposition to <a href="/wiki/FDR" class="mw-redirect" title="FDR">FDR</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> colored the paper's <a href="/wiki/Editorial" title="Editorial">editorial</a> stance as well as its news coverage, which included <a href="/wiki/Opinion_piece" title="Opinion piece">editorializing</a> "news" stories written by Meyer under an assumed name.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> His wife <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Ernst_Meyer" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnes Ernst Meyer">Agnes Ernst Meyer</a> was a journalist from the other end of the spectrum politically. The <i>Post</i> ran many of her pieces including tributes to personal friends <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saul_Alinsky" title="Saul Alinsky">Saul Alinsky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Post</i> is credited with inventing the term "<a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthyism</a>" in a 1950 <a href="/wiki/Editorial_cartoon" title="Editorial cartoon">editorial cartoon</a> by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Block" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Block">Herbert Block</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Depicting buckets of tar, it made fun of Sen. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a>'s "tarring" tactics, i.e., <a href="/wiki/Smear_campaign" title="Smear campaign">smear campaigns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Character_assassination" title="Character assassination">character assassination</a> against those targeted by his accusations. Sen. McCarthy was attempting to do for the Senate what the <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Un-American Activities Committee</a> had been doing for years — investigating <a href="/wiki/Soviet_espionage_in_the_United_States" title="Soviet espionage in the United States">
communist agents</a> in America. The <a href="/wiki/HUAC" class="mw-redirect" title="HUAC">HUAC</a> had evolved from the <a href="/wiki/John_William_McCormack" class="mw-redirect" title="John William McCormack">McCormack</a>-<a href="/wiki/Samuel_Dickstein_(congressman)" title="Samuel Dickstein (congressman)">Dickstein</a> Committee of the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1950s saw the developing association of Phil and <a href="/wiki/Kay_Graham" class="mw-redirect" title="Kay Graham">Kay Graham</a> with the <a href="/wiki/JFK" class="mw-redirect" title="JFK">Kennedys</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bradlee" title="Ben Bradlee">Bradlees</a> and the rest of the "<a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_D.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Georgetown, D.C.">Georgetown</a> Set" (many <a href="/wiki/Harvard" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard">Harvard</a> <a href="/wiki/Alumni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumni">alumni</a>) that would color the <i>Post's</i> political orientation for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phil_Graham" title="Phil Graham">Phil Graham</a>'s friendship with <a href="/wiki/JFK" class="mw-redirect" title="JFK">JFK</a> remained strong until their untimely deaths in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Director_of_the_Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Director of the Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI Director</a> <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> reportedly told the new President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>, "I don't have much influence with the <i>Post</i> because I frankly don't read it. I view it like the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Worker" title="Daily Worker">Daily Worker</a></i>."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ben_Bradlee" title="Ben Bradlee">Ben Bradlee</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Editor-in-chief" title="Editor-in-chief">editor-in-chief</a> in 1968, and <a href="/wiki/Kay_Graham" class="mw-redirect" title="Kay Graham">Kay Graham</a> officially became the publisher in 1969, paving the way for the aggressive reporting of the <i><a href="/wiki/Pentagon_Papers" title="Pentagon Papers">Pentagon Papers</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate scandals</a>. In the mid-1970s, some conservatives referred to the <i>Post</i> as "<i><a href="/wiki/Pravda" title="Pravda">Pravda</a></i> on the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac</a>" because of its perceived left-wing bias in both reporting and editorials.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Since then, the appellation has been used by both liberal and conservative critics of the newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2000.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2000–present">2000–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2000–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i>Buying the War</i> on PBS, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Moyers" title="Bill Moyers">Bill Moyers</a> noted 27 editorials supporting <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>'s ambitions to <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invade Iraq</a>. National security correspondent <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pincus" title="Walter Pincus">Walter Pincus</a> reported that he had been ordered to cease his reports that were critical of Republican administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> According to author and journalist <a href="/wiki/Greg_Mitchell" title="Greg Mitchell">Greg Mitchell</a>, "By the <i>Post</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> own admission, in the months before the war, it ran more than 140 stories on its front page promoting the war, while contrary information 'got lost', as one <i>Post</i> staffer told <a href="/wiki/Howard_Kurtz" title="Howard Kurtz">Kurtz</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 26, 2007, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Matthews" title="Chris Matthews">Chris Matthews</a> said on his television program, "Well, <i>The Washington Post</i> is not the liberal newspaper it was, Congressman, let me tell you. I have been reading it for years and it is a <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">neocon</a> newspaper".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It has regularly published an ideological mixture of op-ed columnists, some of them left-leaning (including <a href="/wiki/E._J._Dionne" title="E. J. Dionne">E.&#160;J. Dionne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dana_Milbank" title="Dana Milbank">Dana Milbank</a>, Greg Sargent, and <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Robinson_(journalist)" title="Eugene Robinson (journalist)">Eugene Robinson</a>), and many on the right (including <a href="/wiki/George_Will" title="George Will">George Will</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Thiessen" title="Marc Thiessen">Marc Thiessen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Gerson" title="Michael Gerson">Michael Gerson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Krauthammer" title="Charles Krauthammer">Charles Krauthammer</a>, who continued writing columns until shortly before his death in 2018).
</p><p>In a study published on April 18, 2007, by Yale professors Alan Gerber, Dean Karlan, and Daniel Bergan, citizens were given a subscription to either the conservative-leaning <i>Washington Times</i> or the liberal-leaning <i>Washington Post</i> to see the effect that media has on voting patterns. Gerber had estimated based on his work that the <i>Post</i> slanted as much to the left as the <i>Times</i> did to the right. Gerber found those who were given a free subscription of the <i>Post</i> were 7.9–11.4% more likely to vote for the Democratic candidate for governor than those assigned to the control group, depending on the adjustment for the date on which individual participants were surveyed and the survey interviewer; surprisingly, however, people who received the <i>Times</i> were also more likely than controls to vote for the Democrat, with an effect approximately 60% as large as that estimated for the <i>Post</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gerber_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gerber-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The study authors noted that sampling error might have played a role in the effect of the conservative-leaning <i>Times</i>, as might the fact that the Democratic candidate took more conservative-leaning positions than is typical for his party, and "the month prior to the post-election survey was a difficult period for President Bush, one in which his overall approval rating fell by approximately 4 percentage points nationwide. It appears that heightened exposure to both papers’ news coverage, despite opposing ideological slants, moved public opinion away from Republicans."<sup id="cite_ref-Gerber_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gerber-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2007, the newspaper was criticized by independent journalist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Parry_(journalist)" title="Robert Parry (journalist)">Robert Parry</a> for reporting on anti-Obama chain e-mails without sufficiently emphasizing to its readers the false nature of the anonymous claims.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, Parry criticized the newspaper for its allegedly unfair reporting on liberal politicians, including Vice President <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> and President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Responding to criticism of the newspaper's coverage during the run-up to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008 presidential election</a>, former <i>Post</i> <a href="/wiki/Ombudsman" title="Ombudsman">ombudsman</a> <a href="/wiki/Deborah_Howell" title="Deborah Howell">Deborah Howell</a> wrote: "The opinion pages have strong conservative voices; the editorial board includes centrists and conservatives; and there were editorials critical of Obama. Yet opinion was still weighted toward Obama."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2009 Oxford University Press book by Richard Davis on the impact of blogs on American politics, liberal bloggers link to <i>The Washington Post</i> and <i>The New York Times</i> more often than other major newspapers; however, conservative bloggers also link predominantly to liberal newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In mid-September 2016, Matthew Ingram of <i>Forbes</i> joined Glenn Greenwald of <i>the Intercept</i>, and Trevor Trimm of <i>The Guardian</i> in cricitizing <i>The Washington Post</i> for  "demanding that [former National Security Agency contractor Edward] Snowden&#160;... stand trial on espionage charges".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2016, The <i>Post</i> published a story inaccurately stating that a <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cyberwarfare" title="Cyberwarfare">hacking operation</a> had infiltrated the <a href="/wiki/Continental_U.S._power_transmission_grid" title="Continental U.S. power transmission grid">U.S. electrical grid</a>; the claim was retracted in a revised version of the story, after the initial version had been widely circulated.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2017, amid a barrage of criticism from <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> over the paper's coverage of his campaign and early presidency as well as concerns among the American press about Trump's criticism and threats against journalists who provide coverage he deems unfavorable, the <i>Post</i> adopted the slogan "Democracy Dies in Darkness" for its masthead.<sup id="cite_ref-OldSaying_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OldSaying-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_endorsements">Political endorsements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Political endorsements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Katharine Graham wrote in her autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_History" title="Personal History">Personal History</a></i> that the newspaper long had a policy of not making endorsements for political candidates. However, since at least 2000, the newspaper has occasionally endorsed Republican politicians, such as <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Maryland" title="Governor of Maryland">Maryland Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ehrlich" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Ehrlich">Robert Ehrlich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, it repeated its historic endorsements of every Republican incumbent for Congress in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Virginia" title="Northern Virginia">Northern Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> There have also been times when the <i>Post</i> has specifically chosen not to endorse any candidate, such as in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1988" title="United States presidential election, 1988">1988 presidential election</a> when it refused to endorse then-Governor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">Michael Dukakis</a> or then-Vice President <a href="/wiki/George_H.W._Bush" class="mw-redirect" title="George H.W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> On October 17, 2008, the <i>Post</i> endorsed <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> On October 25, 2012, the newspaper endorsed the re-election of Barack Obama.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The Post has endorsed Democrats for president during at least nine different presidential elections.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  The paper has never endorsed a Republican for president.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> On October 21, 2014, the newspaper endorsed 44 Democratic candidates versus 3 Republican candidates for the 2014 elections in the District of Columbia, Maryland and Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> On October 13, 2016, it endorsed <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">presidential election</a> of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Post'</i>s early endorsements in the 1978 elections for Maryland Governor (reformist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hughes" title="Harry Hughes">Harry Hughes</a>) and for D.C. Mayor (a young <a href="/wiki/Marion_Barry" title="Marion Barry">Marion Barry</a>) allowed those candidates to tout their endorsements, thereby distinguishing them from an otherwise crowded field of big name candidates.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_controversies">Criticism and controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Criticism and controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id=".22Jimmy.27s_World.22_fabrication"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Jimmy's_World&quot;_fabrication">"Jimmy's World" fabrication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: &quot;Jimmy&#039;s World&quot; fabrication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
In September 1980, a Sunday feature story appeared on the front page of the <i>Post</i> titled "Jimmy's World" in which reporter <a href="/wiki/Janet_Cooke" title="Janet Cooke">Janet Cooke</a> wrote a profile of the life of an eight-year-old <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a> <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">addict</a>. Although some within the <i>Post</i> doubted the story's veracity, the paper's editors defended it, and assistant managing editor <a href="/wiki/Bob_Woodward" title="Bob Woodward">Bob Woodward</a> submitted the story for the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>. Cooke was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_Feature_Writing" title="Pulitzer Prize for Feature Writing">Pulitzer Prize for Feature Writing</a> on April 13, 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The story was then found to be a complete fabrication, and the Pulitzer was returned. In retrospect, Woodward made the following statement:</p><blockquote><p>
I think that the decision to nominate the story for a Pulitzer is of minimal consequence. I also think that it won is of little consequence. It is a brilliant story—fake and fraud that it is. It would be absurd for me or any other editor to review the authenticity or accuracy of stories that are nominated for prizes.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PropOrNot_article">PropOrNot article</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: PropOrNot article">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 2016, the <i>Post</i> published a story that relied heavily on a report by <a href="/wiki/PropOrNot" title="PropOrNot">PropOrNot</a>, an anonymous internet group that seeks to expose what it calls <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>. PropOrNot published a list of websites they called "bona-fide 'useful idiots<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>" of the Russian government.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cockburn" title="Andrew Cockburn">Andrew Cockburn</a>, Washington editor for <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Harper&#39;s">Harper's</a></i>, was sharply critical of <i>Post</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s decision to put the story on its front page, calling the article a "sorry piece of trash".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Writers in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Intercept" title="The Intercept">The Intercept</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> also criticized <i>Post</i> for including a report by an organization with no reputation for fact-checking in an article on "fake news".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Looking more carefully into their methodology, <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Chen" title="Adrian Chen">Adrian Chen</a>, staff writer for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, argued that PropOrNot's criteria for establishing propaganda were so broad that they could have included "not only Russian state-controlled media organizations, such as <i>Russia Today</i>, but nearly every news outlet in the world, including the <i>Post</i> itself" on their list.<sup id="cite_ref-Chen-Prop-about-Prop_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen-Prop-about-Prop-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pay_practices">Pay practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pay practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 2018, over 400 employees of the <i>Washington Post</i> signed an open letter to owner <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a> demanding "fair wages; fair benefits for retirement, family leave and health care; and a fair amount of job security." The open letter was accompanied by video testimonials from employees, who alleged 'shocking pay practices' despite record growth in subscriptions at the newspaper, with salaries only rising an average of $10 per week, which is less than half the rate of inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The petition followed on a year of unsuccessful negotiations between the <i>Washington Post</i> <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">Guild</a> and upper management over pay and benefit increases.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Executive_officers_and_editors_.28past_and_present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_officers_and_editors_(past_and_present)">Executive officers and editors (past and present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Executive officers and editors (past and present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Washington_Post&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Kelly, Tom. <i>The imperial Post: The Meyers, the Grahams, and the paper that rules Washington</i> (Morrow, 1983)</li>
<li>Lewis, Norman P. "Morning Miracle. Inside the Washington Post: A Great Newspaper Fights for Its Life". <i>Journalism and Mass Communication Quarterly</i> (2011) 88#1 pp: 219.</li>
<li>Merrill, John C. and Harold A. Fisher. <i>The world's great dailies: profiles of fifty newspapers</i> (1980) pp 342–52</li>
